				<div class="minerva__subtitle"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel_Corp.&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Intel Corp.">Intel Corp.</a>)</span></div>

			<div id="bodyContent" class="content"><div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="mw-parser-output"><section class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the company. For the information gathering term, see <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_assessment" title="Intelligence assessment">Intelligence assessment</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Intel_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Intel (disambiguation)">Intel (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p>

<span style="font-size: small;"><span id="coordinates"><a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">Coordinates</a>: <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Intel&amp;params=37_23_16_N_121_57_49_W_type:landmark"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">37°23′16″N</span> <span class="longitude">121°57′49″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">37.38778°N 121.96361°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">37.38778; -121.96361</span></span></span></a></span></span></span>
</p>
<p><b>Intel Corporation</b> is an American <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational corporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technology_company" title="Technology company">technology company</a> headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Santa_Clara,_California" title="Santa Clara, California">Santa Clara</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a>. It is the world's largest and highest valued <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_chip" class="mw-redirect" title="Semiconductor chip">semiconductor chip</a> manufacturer based on revenue,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and is the inventor of the <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86</a> series of <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">microprocessors</a>, the processors found in most personal computers (PCs). Intel ranked No. 46 in the 2018 <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500"><i>Fortune</i> 500</a> list of the largest United States corporations by total revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Intel is incorporated in Delaware.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p><table class="infobox vcard" href="#cite_note-xbrlus_1-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Equity_(finance)" title="Equity (finance)">Total (2019)<sup id="cite_ref-xbrlus_1_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xbrlus_1-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Number of employees</div></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;">110,200 (2019)<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Subsidiary" title="Subsidiary">Subsidiaries</a></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mobileye" title="Mobileye">Mobileye</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/McAfee" title="McAfee">McAfee</a> (49%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Here_(company)" class="mw-redirect" title="Here (company)">Here</a> (15%)</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Website</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://intel.com">intel<wbr></wbr>.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table><p>Intel supplies processors for <a href="/wiki/List_of_computer_system_manufacturers" title="List of computer system manufacturers">computer system manufacturers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lenovo" title="Lenovo">Lenovo</a>, <a href="/wiki/HP_Inc." title="HP Inc.">HP</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dell_Technologies" title="Dell Technologies">Dell</a>. Intel also manufactures <a href="/wiki/Motherboard" title="Motherboard">motherboard</a> <a href="/wiki/Chipset" title="Chipset">chipsets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Network_interface_controller" title="Network interface controller">network interface controllers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">integrated circuits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flash_memory" title="Flash memory">flash memory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graphics_processing_unit" title="Graphics processing unit">graphics chips</a>, <a href="/wiki/Embedded_system" title="Embedded system">embedded processors</a> and other devices related to communications and computing.
</p><p>Intel Corporation was founded on July 18, 1968, by semiconductor pioneers <a href="/wiki/Robert_Noyce" title="Robert Noyce">Robert Noyce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Moore" title="Gordon Moore">Gordon Moore</a> (of <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore's law">Moore's law</a>), and is associated with the executive leadership and vision of <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Grove" title="Andrew Grove">Andrew Grove</a>. The company's name was conceived as <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">portmanteau</a> of the words <i>int</i>egrated and <i>el</i>ectronics, with co-founder Noyce having been a key inventor of the <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit#Invention" title="Integrated circuit">integrated circuit</a> (the microchip). The fact that "intel" is the term for intelligence information also made the name appropriate.<sup id="cite_ref-Inquirer_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Inquirer-7">[7]</a></sup> Intel was an early developer of <a href="/wiki/Static_random-access_memory" title="Static random-access memory">SRAM</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_random-access_memory" title="Dynamic random-access memory">DRAM</a> memory chips, which represented the majority of its business until 1981. Although Intel created the world's first commercial microprocessor chip in 1971, it was not until the success of the <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computer</a> (PC) that this became its primary business.
</p><p>During the 1990s, Intel invested heavily in new microprocessor designs fostering the rapid growth of the <a href="/wiki/Computer_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer industry">computer industry</a>. During this period Intel became the <a href="/wiki/Market_dominance" class="mw-redirect" title="Market dominance">dominant</a> supplier of microprocessors for PCs and was known for aggressive and anti-competitive tactics in defense of its market position, particularly against <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Micro_Devices" title="Advanced Micro Devices">Advanced Micro Devices</a> (AMD), as well as a struggle with <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> for control over the direction of the PC industry.<sup id="cite_ref-CNET_September_23,_1998_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNET_September_23,_1998-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_December_14,_1998_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_December_14,_1998-9">[9]</a></sup></p><p>The Open Source Technology Center at Intel hosts <a href="/wiki/PowerTOP" title="PowerTOP">PowerTOP</a> and <a href="/wiki/LatencyTOP" title="LatencyTOP">LatencyTOP</a>, and supports other open-source projects such as <a href="/wiki/Wayland_(display_server_protocol)" title="Wayland (display server protocol)">Wayland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesa3D" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesa3D">Mesa3D</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intel_Array_Building_Blocks" title="Intel Array Building Blocks">Intel Array Building Blocks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Threading_Building_Blocks" title="Threading Building Blocks">Threading Building Blocks</a> (TBB), and <a href="/wiki/Xen" title="Xen">Xen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_operations">Current operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Current operations" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1">
<table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2019</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table><h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Operating_segments">Operating segments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Operating segments" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<ul><li><b>Client Computing Group</b> –  55% of 2016 revenues –  produces hardware components used in desktop and notebook computers.<sup id="cite_ref-10K_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Data Center Group</b> –  29% of 2016 revenues –  produces hardware components used in server, network, and storage platforms.<sup id="cite_ref-10K_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Internet of Things Group</b> –  5% of 2016 revenues –  offers platforms designed for retail, transportation, industrial, buildings and home use.<sup id="cite_ref-10K_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Non-Volatile Memory Solutions Group</b> –  4% of 2016 revenues –  manufactures <a href="/wiki/NAND_flash_memory" class="mw-redirect" title="NAND flash memory">NAND flash memory</a> and <a href="/wiki/3D_XPoint" title="3D XPoint">3D XPoint</a>, branded as <a href="/wiki/Optane" class="mw-redirect" title="Optane">Optane</a>, products primarily used in <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_drive" title="Solid-state drive">solid-state drives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10K_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Intel Security Group</b> –  4% of 2016 revenues –  produces software, particularly security, and <a href="/wiki/Antivirus" class="mw-redirect" title="Antivirus">antivirus</a> software.<sup id="cite_ref-10K_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Programmable Solutions Group</b> –  3% of 2016 revenues –  manufactures programmable semiconductors (primarily <a href="/wiki/FPGA" class="mw-redirect" title="FPGA">FPGAs</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-10K_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-11">[11]</a></sup></li></ul><h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Top_customers">Top customers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Top customers" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2017, <a href="/wiki/Dell" title="Dell">Dell</a> accounted for about 16% of Intel's total revenues, <a href="/wiki/Lenovo" title="Lenovo">Lenovo</a> accounted for 13% of total revenues, and <a href="/wiki/HP_Inc." title="HP Inc.">HP Inc.</a> accounted for 11% of total revenues.<sup id="cite_ref-10K_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_share">Market share</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Market share" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_share_in_early_2011">Market share in early 2011</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Market share in early 2011" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/International_Data_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="International Data Corporation">IDC</a>, while Intel enjoyed the biggest market share in both the overall worldwide PC microprocessor market (73.3%) and the mobile PC microprocessor (80.4%) in the second quarter of 2011, the numbers decreased by 1.5% and 1.9% compared to the first quarter of 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_status">Current status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Current status" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Intels market share decreased significantly in the enthusiast market as of 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Intel has faced delays for their 10 nm products.According to Intel CEO Bob Swan, that delay was caused by the company's overly aggressive strategy for moving to its next node.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Some OEMs,for example Microsoft, started newly shipping products with AMD CPUs.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_market_share">Historical market share</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Historical market share" data-section="7" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>In the 1980s Intel was among the top ten sellers of <a href="/wiki/Semiconductors" class="mw-redirect" title="Semiconductors">semiconductors</a> (10th in 1987) in the world. In 1992,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Intel became the biggest chip maker by revenue and has held the position ever since. Other top semiconductor companies include <a href="/wiki/TSMC" title="TSMC">TSMC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Micro_Devices" title="Advanced Micro Devices">Advanced Micro Devices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samsung" title="Samsung">Samsung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas_Instruments" title="Texas Instruments">Texas Instruments</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toshiba" title="Toshiba">Toshiba</a> and <a href="/wiki/STMicroelectronics" title="STMicroelectronics">STMicroelectronics</a>.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_competitors">Major competitors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Major competitors" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Competitors in PC chipsets include <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Micro_Devices" title="Advanced Micro Devices">Advanced Micro Devices</a>, <a href="/wiki/VIA_Technologies" title="VIA Technologies">VIA Technologies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Integrated_Systems" title="Silicon Integrated Systems">Silicon Integrated Systems</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nvidia" title="Nvidia">Nvidia</a>. Intel's competitors in networking include <a href="/wiki/NXP_Semiconductors" title="NXP Semiconductors">NXP Semiconductors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infineon" class="mw-redirect" title="Infineon">Infineon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Broadcom_Limited" class="mw-redirect" title="Broadcom Limited">Broadcom Limited</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marvell_Technology_Group" title="Marvell Technology Group">Marvell Technology Group</a> and <a href="/wiki/Applied_Micro_Circuits_Corporation" title="Applied Micro Circuits Corporation">Applied Micro Circuits Corporation</a>, and competitors in flash memory include <a href="/wiki/Spansion" title="Spansion">Spansion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samsung" title="Samsung">Samsung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qimonda" title="Qimonda">Qimonda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toshiba" title="Toshiba">Toshiba</a>, <a href="/wiki/STMicroelectronics" title="STMicroelectronics">STMicroelectronics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/SK_Hynix" title="SK Hynix">SK Hynix</a>.
</p><p>The only major competitor in the <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86</a> processor market is <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Micro_Devices" title="Advanced Micro Devices">Advanced Micro Devices</a> (AMD), with which Intel has had full cross-licensing agreements since 1976: each partner can use the other's patented technological innovations without charge after a certain time.<sup id="cite_ref-Intel-AMD_deal_2001_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intel-AMD_deal_2001-18">[18]</a></sup> However, the cross-licensing agreement is canceled in the event of an AMD bankruptcy or takeover.<sup id="cite_ref-Patent_Cross_License_Agreement_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patent_Cross_License_Agreement-19">[19]</a></sup></p><p>Some smaller competitors such as <a href="/wiki/VIA_Technologies" title="VIA Technologies">VIA Technologies</a> produce <a href="/wiki/Low-power_electronics" title="Low-power electronics">low-power</a> <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86</a> processors for small factor computers and portable equipment. However, the advent of such mobile computing devices, in particular, <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a>, has in recent years led to a decline in PC sales.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Since over 95% of the world's smartphones currently use processors designed by <a href="/wiki/ARM_Holdings" class="mw-redirect" title="ARM Holdings">ARM Holdings</a>, ARM has become a major competitor for Intel's processor market. ARM is also planning to make inroads into the PC and server market.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p><p>Intel has been involved in several disputes regarding violation of <a href="/wiki/Antitrust_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Antitrust laws">antitrust laws</a>, which are noted below.
</p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(2)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_history">Corporate history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Corporate history" data-section="9" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-2 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-2">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Intel" title="Timeline of Intel">Timeline of Intel</a></div>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Origins" data-section="10" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andy_Grove_Robert_Noyce_Gordon_Moore_1978_edit.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Andy_Grove_Robert_Noyce_Gordon_Moore_1978_edit.jpg/170px-Andy_Grove_Robert_Noyce_Gordon_Moore_1978_edit.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="221" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="990" data-file-height="1288"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 170px;height: 221px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Andy_Grove_Robert_Noyce_Gordon_Moore_1978_edit.jpg/170px-Andy_Grove_Robert_Noyce_Gordon_Moore_1978_edit.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="170" data-height="221" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andy_Grove_Robert_Noyce_Gordon_Moore_1978_edit.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Andy_Grove" class="mw-redirect" title="Andy Grove">Andy Grove</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Noyce" title="Robert Noyce">Robert Noyce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Moore" title="Gordon Moore">Gordon Moore</a> in 1978</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Intel_old_logo.svg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Intel_old_logo.svg/110px-Intel_old_logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="110" height="57" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="730" data-file-height="381"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 110px;height: 57px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Intel_old_logo.svg/110px-Intel_old_logo.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="110" data-height="57" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Intel_old_logo.svg/165px-Intel_old_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Intel_old_logo.svg/220px-Intel_old_logo.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Intel_old_logo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Logo used from 1968 to 2006</div></div></div>
<p>Intel was founded in <a href="/wiki/Mountain_View,_California" title="Mountain View, California">Mountain View, California</a>, in 1968 by <a href="/wiki/Gordon_E._Moore" class="mw-redirect" title="Gordon E. Moore">Gordon E. Moore</a> (known for "<a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore's law">Moore's law</a>"), a <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Noyce" title="Robert Noyce">Robert Noyce</a>, a physicist and co-inventor of the <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">integrated circuit</a>. <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rock" title="Arthur Rock">Arthur Rock</a> (investor and <a href="/wiki/Venture_capital" title="Venture capital">venture capitalist</a>) helped them find investors, while <a href="/wiki/Max_Palevsky" title="Max Palevsky">Max Palevsky</a> was on the board from an early stage.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Moore and Noyce had left <a href="/wiki/Fairchild_Semiconductor" title="Fairchild Semiconductor">Fairchild Semiconductor</a> to found Intel. Rock was not an employee, but he was an investor and was <a href="/wiki/Chairman" class="mw-redirect" title="Chairman">chairman of the board</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Intel_Museum_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intel_Museum-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The total initial investment in Intel was $2.5 million in convertible debentures (equivalent to $18.4 million in 2019) and $10,000 from Rock. Just 2 years later, Intel became a <a href="/wiki/Public_company" title="Public company">public company</a> via an <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offering</a> (IPO), raising $6.8 million ($23.50 per share).<sup id="cite_ref-Intel_Museum_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intel_Museum-23">[23]</a></sup> Intel's third employee was <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Grove" title="Andrew Grove">Andy Grove</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_engineer" title="Chemical engineer">chemical engineer</a>, who later ran the company through much of the 1980s and the high-growth 1990s.
</p><p>In deciding on a name, Moore and Noyce quickly rejected "Moore Noyce",<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> near <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/homophone" class="extiw" title="wikt:homophone">homophone</a> for "more noise" – an ill-suited name for an <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a> company, since <a href="/wiki/Electronic_noise" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic noise">noise in electronics</a> is usually undesirable and typically associated with bad <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_interference" title="Electromagnetic interference">interference</a>. Instead, they founded the company as <b>N M Electronics</b> on July 18, 1968, but by the end of the month had changed the name to <b>Intel</b> which stood for <b>Int</b>egrated <b>El</b>ectronics.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[note 1]</a></sup> Since "Intel" was already trademarked by the hotel chain Intelco, they had to buy the rights for the name.<sup id="cite_ref-Intel_Museum_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intel_Museum-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Early history" data-section="11" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>At its founding, Intel was distinguished by its ability to make <a href="/wiki/Logic_circuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Logic circuit">logic circuits</a> using <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_device" title="Semiconductor device">semiconductor devices</a>. The founders' goal was the <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_memory" title="Semiconductor memory">semiconductor memory</a> market, widely predicted to replace <a href="/wiki/Magnetic-core_memory" title="Magnetic-core memory">magnetic-core memory</a>. Its first product, a quick entry into the small, high-speed memory market in 1969, was the 3101 <a href="/wiki/Schottky_transistor" title="Schottky transistor">Schottky TTL</a> <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_junction_transistor" title="Bipolar junction transistor">bipolar</a> 64-bit <a href="/wiki/Static_random-access_memory" title="Static random-access memory">static random-access memory</a> (SRAM), which was nearly twice as fast as earlier Schottky diode implementations by Fairchild and the Electrotechnical Laboratory in <a href="/wiki/Tsukuba,_Ibaraki" title="Tsukuba, Ibaraki">Tsukuba, Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> In the same year, Intel also produced the 3301 Schottky bipolar 1024-bit <a href="/wiki/Read-only_memory" title="Read-only memory">read-only memory</a> (ROM)<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> and the first commercial <a href="/wiki/MOSFET" title="MOSFET">metal–oxide–semiconductor field-effect transistor</a> (MOSFET) <a href="/wiki/Silicon_gate" class="mw-redirect" title="Silicon gate">silicon gate</a> SRAM chip, the 256-bit 1101.<sup id="cite_ref-Intel_Museum_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intel_Museum-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Intel-Product-Timeline_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intel-Product-Timeline-37">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> While the 1101 was a significant advance, its complex static <a href="/wiki/Memory_cell_(computing)" title="Memory cell (computing)">cell structure</a> made it too slow and costly for <a href="/wiki/Mainframe_computer" title="Mainframe computer">mainframe</a> memories. The three-<a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistor</a> cell implemented in the first commercially available <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_random-access_memory" title="Dynamic random-access memory">dynamic random-access memory</a> (DRAM), the <a href="/wiki/Intel_1103" title="Intel 1103">1103</a> released in 1970, solved these issues. The 1103 was the bestselling semiconductor memory chip in the world by 1972, as it replaced core memory in many applications.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bellis_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bellis-40">[39]</a></sup> Intel's business grew during the 1970s as it expanded and improved its manufacturing processes and produced a wider range of <a href="/wiki/Product_(business)" title="Product (business)">products</a>, still dominated by various memory devices.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Federico_Faggin_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Federico_Faggin_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Federico_Faggin_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1377" data-file-height="1732"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 170px;height: 214px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Federico_Faggin_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Federico_Faggin_%28cropped%29.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="170" data-height="214" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Federico_Faggin_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Federico_Faggin" title="Federico Faggin">Federico Faggin</a>, designer of the <a href="/wiki/Intel_4004" title="Intel 4004">Intel 4004</a></div></div></div>
<p>Intel created the <a href="/wiki/Intel_4004" title="Intel 4004">first commercially available microprocessor (Intel 4004)</a> in 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-Intel_Museum_23-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intel_Museum-23">[23]</a></sup> The microprocessor represented a notable advance in the technology of integrated circuitry, as it miniaturized the central processing unit of a computer, which then made it possible for small machines to perform calculations that in the past only very large machines could do. Considerable technological innovation was needed before the microprocessor could actually become the basis of what was first known as a "mini computer" and then known as a "personal computer".<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Intel also created one of the first <a href="/wiki/Microcomputer" title="Microcomputer">microcomputers</a> in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-Intel-Product-Timeline_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intel-Product-Timeline-37">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Intellec-1973_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intellec-1973-42">[41]</a></sup> Intel opened its first international manufacturing facility in 1972, in <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, which would host multiple Intel operations, before opening assembly facilities and semiconductor plants in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> in the early 1980s, and manufacturing and development centres in China, India and Costa Rica in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> By the early 1980s, its business was dominated by <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_random-access_memory" title="Dynamic random-access memory">dynamic random-access memory</a> (DRAM) chips. However, increased competition from Japanese semiconductor manufacturers had, by 1983, dramatically reduced the profitability of this market. The growing success of the <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> personal computer, based on an Intel microprocessor, was among factors that convinced Gordon Moore (CEO since 1975) to shift the company's focus to microprocessors and to change fundamental aspects of that business model. Moore's decision to sole-source Intel's 386 chip played into the company's continuing success.
</p><p>By the end of the 1980s, buoyed by its fortuitous position as microprocessor supplier to IBM and IBM's competitors within the rapidly growing <a href="/wiki/IBM_PC_compatible" title="IBM PC compatible">personal computer market</a>, Intel embarked on a 10-year period of unprecedented growth as the primary (and most profitable) hardware supplier to the PC industry, part of the winning 'Wintel' combination. Moore handed over to Andy Grove in 1987. By launching its Intel Inside <a href="/wiki/Marketing_campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Marketing campaign">marketing campaign</a> in 1991, Intel was able to associate <a href="/wiki/Brand_loyalty" title="Brand loyalty">brand loyalty</a> with consumer selection, so that by the end of the 1990s, its line of <a href="/wiki/Pentium" title="Pentium">Pentium</a> processors had become a household name.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Slowing_demand_and_challenges_to_dominance_in_2000">Slowing demand and challenges to dominance in 2000</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Slowing demand and challenges to dominance in 2000" data-section="12" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>After 2000, growth in demand for high-end microprocessors slowed. Competitors, notably <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Micro_Devices" title="Advanced Micro Devices">AMD</a> (Intel's largest competitor in its primary <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86 architecture</a> market), garnered significant market share, initially in low-end and mid-range processors but ultimately across the product range, and Intel's dominant position in its core market was greatly reduced,<sup id="cite_ref-Wong_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wong-44">[43]</a></sup> mostly due to controversional <a href="/wiki/NetBurst_(microarchitecture)" title="NetBurst (microarchitecture)">NetBurst</a> microarchitecture. In the early 2000s then-CEO, <a href="/wiki/Craig_Barrett_(businessman)" class="mw-redirect" title="Craig Barrett (businessman)">Craig Barrett</a> attempted to diversify the company's business beyond semiconductors, but few of these activities were ultimately successful.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Litigation">Litigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Litigation" data-section="13" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Intel had also for a number of years been embroiled in litigation. US law did not initially recognize <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Intellectual property rights">intellectual property rights</a> related to microprocessor <a href="/wiki/Topology_(electrical_circuits)" title="Topology (electrical circuits)">topology</a> (circuit layouts), until the <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_Chip_Protection_Act_of_1984" title="Semiconductor Chip Protection Act of 1984">Semiconductor Chip Protection Act of 1984</a>, a law sought by Intel and the Semiconductor Industry Association (SIA).<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> During the late 1980s and 1990s (after this law was passed), Intel also sued companies that tried to develop competitor chips to the <a href="/wiki/Intel_80386" title="Intel 80386">80386</a> <a href="/wiki/CPU" class="mw-redirect" title="CPU">CPU</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-46">[45]</a></sup> The lawsuits were noted to significantly burden the competition with legal bills, even if Intel lost the suits.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-46">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Antitrust" class="mw-redirect" title="Antitrust">Antitrust</a> allegations had been simmering since the early 1990s and had been the cause of one <a href="/wiki/Lawsuit" title="Lawsuit">lawsuit</a> against Intel in 1991. In 2004 and 2005, AMD brought <a href="/wiki/Intel_Corp._v._Advanced_Micro_Devices,_Inc." title="Intel Corp. v. Advanced Micro Devices, Inc.">further claims</a> against Intel related to <a href="/wiki/Unfair_competition" title="Unfair competition">unfair competition</a>.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Regaining_of_momentum_.282005.E2.80.932007.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Regaining_of_momentum_(2005–2007)">Regaining of momentum (2005–2007)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Regaining of momentum (2005–2007)" data-section="14" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2005, CEO <a href="/wiki/Paul_Otellini" title="Paul Otellini">Paul Otellini</a> reorganized the company to refocus its core processor and chipset business on platforms (enterprise, digital home, digital health, and mobility).
</p><p>In 2006, Intel unveiled its <a href="/wiki/Core_(microarchitecture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Core (microarchitecture)">Core microarchitecture</a> to widespread critical acclaim;<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> the product range was perceived as an exceptional leap in processor performance that at a stroke regained much of its leadership of the field.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> In 2008, Intel had another "tick" when it introduced the Penryn microarchitecture, which was 45 nm. Later that year, Intel released a processor with the <a href="/wiki/Nehalem_(microarchitecture)" title="Nehalem (microarchitecture)">Nehalem</a> architecture. Nehalem had positive reviews.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Sale_of_XScale_processor_business_.282006.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sale_of_XScale_processor_business_(2006)">Sale of XScale processor business (2006)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Sale of XScale processor business (2006)" data-section="15" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On June 27, 2006, the sale of Intel's <a href="/wiki/XScale" title="XScale">XScale</a> assets was announced. Intel agreed to sell the XScale processor business to <a href="/wiki/Marvell_Technology_Group" title="Marvell Technology Group">Marvell Technology Group</a> for an estimated $600 million and the assumption of unspecified liabilities. The move was intended to permit Intel to focus its resources on its core x86 and server businesses, and the <a href="/wiki/Mergers_and_acquisitions" title="Mergers and acquisitions">acquisition</a> completed on November 9, 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-XScale_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-XScale-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Acquisitions_and_investments_.282010.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisitions_and_investments_(2010–present)">Acquisitions and investments (2010–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Acquisitions and investments (2010–present)" data-section="16" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2010, Intel purchased <a href="/wiki/McAfee" title="McAfee">McAfee</a>, a manufacturer of computer security technology, for $7.68 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> As a condition for regulatory approval of the transaction, Intel agreed to provide rival security firms with all necessary information that would allow their products to use Intel's chips and personal computers.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> After the acquisition, Intel had about 90,000 employees, including about 12,000 software engineers.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj2011_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj2011-54">[53]</a></sup> In September 2016, Intel sold a majority stake in its computer-security unit to <a href="/wiki/TPG_Capital" title="TPG Capital">TPG Capital</a>, reversing the five-year-old McAfee acquisition.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2010, Intel and <a href="/wiki/Infineon_Technologies" title="Infineon Technologies">Infineon Technologies</a> announced that Intel would acquire Infineon's Wireless Solutions business.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> Intel planned to use Infineon's technology in laptops, smart phones, netbooks, tablets and embedded computers in consumer products, eventually integrating its wireless modem into Intel's silicon chips.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup></p><p>In March 2011, Intel bought most of the assets of Cairo-based SySDSoft.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup></p><p>In July 2011, Intel announced that it had agreed to acquire Fulcrum Microsystems Inc., a company specializing in network switches.<sup id="cite_ref-EETimes_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EETimes-59">[58]</a></sup> The company used to be included on the EE Times list of 60 Emerging Startups.<sup id="cite_ref-EETimes_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EETimes-59">[58]</a></sup></p><p>In October 2011, Intel reached a deal to acquire <a href="/w/index.php?title=Telmap&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Telmap (page does not exist)">Telmap</a>, an Israeli-based navigation software company. The purchase price was not disclosed, but Israeli media reported values around $300 million to $350 million.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup></p><p>In July 2012, Intel agreed to buy 10% of the shares of <a href="/wiki/ASML_Holding" title="ASML Holding">ASML Holding</a> NV for $2.1 billion and another $1 billion for 5% of the shares that need shareholder approval to fund relevant research and development efforts, as part of a EUR3.3 billion ($4.1 billion) deal to accelerate the development of 450-millimeter wafer technology and extreme ultra-violet lithography by as much as two years.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup></p><p>In July 2013, Intel confirmed the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Omek_Interactive" title="Omek Interactive">Omek Interactive</a>, an Israeli company that makes technology for gesture-based interfaces, without disclosing the monetary value of the deal. An official statement from Intel read: "The acquisition of Omek Interactive will help increase Intel's capabilities in the delivery of more immersive perceptual computing experiences." One report estimated the value of the acquisition between US$30 million and $50 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Omek_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Omek-62">[61]</a></sup></p><p>The acquisition of a Spanish <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_recognition" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural language recognition">natural language recognition</a> startup, Indisys was announced in September 2013. The terms of the deal were not disclosed but an email from an Intel representative stated: "Intel has acquired Indisys, a privately held company based in Seville, Spain. The majority of Indisys employees joined Intel. We signed the agreement to acquire the company on May 31 and the deal has been completed." Indysis explains that its artificial intelligence (AI) technology "is a human image, which converses fluently and with common sense in multiple languages and also works in different platforms."<sup id="cite_ref-Ai_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ai-63">[62]</a></sup></p><p>In December 2014, Intel bought PasswordBox.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2015, Intel purchased a 30% stake in Vuzix, a smart glasses manufacturer. The deal was worth $24.8 million.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup></p><p>In February 2015, Intel announced its agreement to purchase German network chipmaker Lantiq, to aid in its expansion of its range of chips in devices with Internet connection capability.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup></p><p>In June 2015, Intel announced its agreement to purchase FPGA design company <a href="/wiki/Altera" title="Altera">Altera</a> for $16.7 billion, in its largest acquisition to date.<sup id="cite_ref-Wall_Street_Journal_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wall_Street_Journal-67">[66]</a></sup> The acquisition completed in December 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup></p><p>In October 2015, Intel bought <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_computing" title="Cognitive computing">cognitive computing</a> company <a href="/wiki/Saffron_Technology" title="Saffron Technology">Saffron Technology</a> for an undisclosed price.<sup id="cite_ref-saffron_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-saffron-69">[68]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2016, Intel purchased deep-learning startup <a href="/wiki/Nervana_Systems" title="Nervana Systems">Nervana Systems</a> for $350 million.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup></p><p>In December 2016, Intel acquired computer vision startup <a href="/wiki/Movidius" title="Movidius">Movidius</a> for an undisclosed price.<sup id="cite_ref-movidius_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-movidius-71">[70]</a></sup></p><p>In March 2017, Intel announced that they had agreed to purchase <a href="/wiki/Mobileye" title="Mobileye">Mobileye</a>, an Israeli developer of "autonomous driving" systems for US$15.3 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-72">[71]</a></sup></p><p>In June 2017, Intel Corporation announced an investment of over Rs.1100 crore ($170 million) for its upcoming Research and Development (R&amp;D) centre in Bangalore.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2019, Intel announced an investment of over $11 billion on a new Israeli chip plant, as told by the Israeli Finance Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup></p>

</td>

<td>Mashery
</td>
