class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INTEL-20200211-147">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Remote_Keyboard_Android_App">Remote Keyboard Android App</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Remote Keyboard Android App" data-section="29" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Intel has decided to discontinue with their recent Intel Remote Keyboard Android app after encountering several security bugs. This app was launched in early 2015 to help users control Intel single-board computers and Intel NUC. The company has asked Remote Keyboard Users to delete the app at their first convenience.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Solid-state_drives_.28SSD.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Solid-state_drives_(SSD)"><span id="SSD"></span>Solid-state drives (SSD)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Solid-state drives (SSD)" data-section="30" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Intel_SSDs" title="List of Intel SSDs">List of Intel SSDs</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Intel_X25-M_Solid-State_Drive.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Intel_X25-M_Solid-State_Drive.jpg/220px-Intel_X25-M_Solid-State_Drive.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3779" data-file-height="2417"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 141px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Intel_X25-M_Solid-State_Drive.jpg/220px-Intel_X25-M_Solid-State_Drive.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="141" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Intel_X25-M_Solid-State_Drive.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Intel_X25-M" class="mw-redirect" title="Intel X25-M">Intel X25-M</a> SSD</div></div></div>
<p>In 2008, Intel began shipping mainstream <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_drive" title="Solid-state drive">solid-state drives</a> (SSDs) with up to 160 GB storage capacities.<sup id="cite_ref-X25-M1_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-X25-M1-149">[148]</a></sup> As with their CPUs, Intel develops SSD chips using ever-smaller nanometer processes. These SSDs make use of industry standards such as <a href="/wiki/NAND_flash" class="mw-redirect" title="NAND flash">NAND flash</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/SATA#mSATA" class="mw-redirect" title="SATA">mSATA</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-SSD310_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSD310-151">[150]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/PCIe" class="mw-redirect" title="PCIe">PCIe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/NVM_Express" title="NVM Express">NVMe</a>. In 2017, Intel introduced SSDs based on <a href="/wiki/3D_XPoint" title="3D XPoint">3D XPoint</a> technology under the Optane brand name.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Supercomputers">Supercomputers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Supercomputers" data-section="31" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>The Intel Scientific Computers division was founded in 1984 by <a href="/wiki/Justin_Rattner" title="Justin Rattner">Justin Rattner</a>, to design and produce <a href="/wiki/Parallel_computing" title="Parallel computing">parallel computers</a> based on Intel microprocessors connected in <a href="/wiki/Hypercube_internetwork_topology" title="Hypercube internetwork topology">hypercube internetwork topology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wilson-1994_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-1994-153">[152]</a></sup> In 1992, the name was changed to the Intel Supercomputing Systems Division, and development of the <a href="/wiki/IWarp" title="IWarp">iWarp</a> architecture was also subsumed.<sup id="cite_ref-cmu-iwarp_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cmu-iwarp-154">[153]</a></sup> The division designed several <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer" title="Supercomputer">supercomputer</a> systems, including the <a href="/wiki/Intel_iPSC" title="Intel iPSC">Intel iPSC</a>/1, <a href="/wiki/Intel_iPSC/2" class="mw-redirect" title="Intel iPSC/2">iPSC/2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intel_iPSC/860" class="mw-redirect" title="Intel iPSC/860">iPSC/860</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intel_Paragon" title="Intel Paragon">Paragon</a> and <a href="/wiki/ASCI_Red" title="ASCI Red">ASCI Red</a>. In November 2014, Intel revealed that it is going to use light beams to speed up supercomputers.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Mobile_Linux_software">Mobile Linux software</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Mobile Linux software" data-section="32" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2007, Intel formed the <a href="/wiki/Moblin" title="Moblin">Moblin</a> project to create an <a href="/wiki/Open-source_software" title="Open-source software">open source</a> <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a> operating system for x86-based mobile devices. Following the success of <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> platform which ran exclusively on <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture" title="ARM architecture">ARM</a> processors, Intel announced on February 15, 2010, that it would partner with <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a> and merge Moblin with Nokia's ARM-based <a href="/wiki/Maemo" title="Maemo">Maemo</a> project to create <a href="/wiki/MeeGo" title="MeeGo">MeeGo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup> MeeGo was supported by the <a href="/wiki/Linux_Foundation" title="Linux Foundation">Linux Foundation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup></p><p>In February 2011, Nokia left the project after partnering with <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>, leaving Intel in sole charge of MeeGo. An Intel spokeswoman said it was "disappointed" by Nokia's decision but that Intel was committed to MeeGo.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> In September 2011 Intel stopped working on MeeGo and partnered with <a href="/wiki/Samsung" title="Samsung">Samsung</a> to create <a href="/wiki/Tizen" title="Tizen">Tizen</a>, a new project hosted by the Linux Foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup> Intel has since been co-developing the Tizen operating system which runs on several Samsung devices.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Competition.2C_antitrust_and_espionage"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition,_antitrust_and_espionage">Competition, antitrust and espionage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Competition, antitrust and espionage" data-section="33" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/AMD_v._Intel" class="mw-redirect" title="AMD v. Intel">AMD v. Intel</a></div>
<p>Two factors combined to end this dominance: the slowing of <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">PC</a> demand growth beginning in 2000 and the rise of the low-cost PC. By the end of the 1990s, <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">microprocessor</a> performance had outstripped software demand for that CPU power. Aside from high-end server systems and software, whose demand dropped with the end of the "<a href="/wiki/Dot-com_bubble" title="Dot-com bubble">dot-com bubble</a>", consumer systems ran effectively on increasingly low-cost systems after 2000. Intel's strategy of producing ever-more-powerful processors and obsoleting their predecessors stumbled,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> leaving an opportunity for rapid gains by competitors, notably AMD. This, in turn, lowered the profitability<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of the processor line and ended an era of unprecedented dominance of the PC hardware by Intel.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>Intel's dominance in the <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86</a> microprocessor market led to numerous charges of <a href="/wiki/Antitrust" class="mw-redirect" title="Antitrust">antitrust</a> violations over the years, including <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">FTC</a> investigations in both the late 1980s and in 1999, and civil actions such as the 1997 suit by <a href="/wiki/Digital_Equipment_Corporation" title="Digital Equipment Corporation">Digital Equipment Corporation</a> (DEC) and a patent suit by <a href="/wiki/Intergraph" title="Intergraph">Intergraph</a>. Intel's market dominance (at one time<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (May 2009)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> it controlled over 85% of the market for 32-bit x86 microprocessors) combined with Intel's own hardball legal tactics (such as its infamous 338 patent suit versus PC manufacturers)<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> made it an attractive target for litigation, but few of the lawsuits ever amounted to anything.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2009)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>A case of <a href="/wiki/Industrial_espionage" title="Industrial espionage">industrial espionage</a> arose in 1995 that involved both Intel and AMD. <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gaede" title="Bill Gaede">Bill Gaede</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentine</a> formerly employed both at AMD and at Intel's <a href="/wiki/Chandler,_Arizona" title="Chandler, Arizona">Arizona</a> plant, was arrested for attempting in 1993 to sell the <a href="/wiki/I486" class="mw-redirect" title="I486">i486</a> and <a href="/wiki/P5_(microarchitecture)" title="P5 (microarchitecture)">P5</a> Pentium designs to AMD and to certain foreign powers.<sup id="cite_ref-intel-spy_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-intel-spy-161">[160]</a></sup> Gaede videotaped data from his computer screen at Intel and mailed it to <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Micro_Devices" title="Advanced Micro Devices">AMD</a>, which immediately alerted Intel and authorities, resulting in Gaede's arrest. Gaede was convicted and sentenced to 33 months in prison in June 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-Guillermo_Gaede_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guillermo_Gaede-162">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guilty_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guilty-163">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Use_of_Intel_products_by_Apple_Computer_.282005.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_Intel_products_by_Apple_Computer_(2005–present)">Use of Intel products by Apple Computer (2005–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Use of Intel products by Apple Computer (2005–present)" data-section="34" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Apple%27s_transition_to_Intel_processors" title="Apple's transition to Intel processors">Apple's transition to Intel processors</a></div>
<p>On June 6, 2005, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jobs" title="Steve Jobs">Steve Jobs</a>, then CEO of <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a>, announced that Apple would be transitioning from its long favored <a href="/wiki/PowerPC" title="PowerPC">PowerPC</a> architecture to the Intel x86 architecture because the future PowerPC road map was unable to satisfy Apple's needs. The first <a href="/wiki/Macintosh" title="Macintosh">Macintosh</a> computers containing Intel CPUs were announced on January 10, 2006, and Apple had its entire line of consumer Macs running on Intel processors by early August 2006. The Apple Xserve server was updated to Intel <a href="/wiki/Xeon" title="Xeon">Xeon</a> processors from November 2006 and was offered in a configuration similar to Apple's Mac Pro.<sup id="cite_ref-newmacs_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newmacs-164">[163]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Core_2_Duo_advertisement_controversy_.282007.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Core_2_Duo_advertisement_controversy_(2007)">Core 2 Duo advertisement controversy (2007)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Core 2 Duo advertisement controversy (2007)" data-section="35" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In July 2007, the company released a print advertisement for its <a href="/wiki/Intel_Core_2" title="Intel Core 2">Intel Core 2</a> Duo processor featuring six black runners appearing to bow down to a Caucasian male inside of an office setting (due to the posture taken by runners on <a href="/wiki/Starting_blocks" title="Starting blocks">starting blocks</a>). According to Nancy Bhagat, Vice President of Intel Corporate Marketing, viewers found the ad to be "insensitive and insulting", and several Intel executives made public apologies.<sup id="cite_ref-Intel-ad_blog_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intel-ad_blog-165">[164]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Introduction_of_Classmate_PC_.282011.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction_of_Classmate_PC_(2011)">Introduction of Classmate PC (2011)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Introduction of Classmate PC (2011)" data-section="36" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Classmate_PC" title="Classmate PC">Classmate PC</a> is the company's first low-cost <a href="/wiki/Netbook" title="Netbook">netbook</a> computer.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup> In 2014, the company released an updated version of the Classmate PC.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Introduction_of_new_mobile_processor_technology_.282011.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction_of_new_mobile_processor_technology_(2011)">Introduction of new mobile processor technology (2011)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Introduction of new mobile processor technology (2011)" data-section="37" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In June 2011, Intel introduced the first Pentium mobile processor based on the <a href="/wiki/Sandy_Bridge" title="Sandy Bridge">Sandy Bridge</a> core. The B940, clocked at 2 GHz, is faster than existing or upcoming mobile Celerons, although it is almost identical to dual-core Celeron CPUs in all other aspects.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup> According to IHS iSuppli's report on September 28, 2011, Sandy Bridge chips have helped Intel increase its market share in global processor market to 81.8%, while AMD's market share dropped to 10.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup></p><p>Intel planned to introduce Medfield – a processor for tablets and smartphones – to the market in 2012, as an effort to compete with ARM.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup> As a 32-nanometer processor, Medfield is designed to be energy-efficient, which is one of the core features in ARM's chips.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-171">[170]</a></sup></p><p>At the Intel Developers Forum (IDF) 2011 in San Francisco, Intel's partnership with Google was announced. By January 2012, Google's Android 2.3 will use Intel's Atom microprocessor.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Update_to_server_chips_.282011.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Update_to_server_chips_(2011)">Update to server chips (2011)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Update to server chips (2011)" data-section="38" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In July 2011, Intel announced that its server chips, the Xeon series, will use new sensors that can improve data center cooling efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[174]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Introduction_of_Ivy_Bridge_22_nm_processors_.282011.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction_of_Ivy_Bridge_22_nm_processors_(2011)">Introduction of Ivy Bridge 22 nm processors (2011)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Introduction of Ivy Bridge 22 nm processors (2011)" data-section="39" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Bridge_(microarchitecture)" title="Ivy Bridge (microarchitecture)">Ivy Bridge (microarchitecture)</a></div>
<p>In 2011, Intel announced the <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Bridge_(microarchitecture)" title="Ivy Bridge (microarchitecture)">Ivy Bridge</a> processor family at the Intel Developer Forum.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup> Ivy Bridge supports both DDR3 memory and DDR3L chips.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Development_of_Personal_Office_Energy_Monitor_.28POEM.29_.282011.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_Personal_Office_Energy_Monitor_(POEM)_(2011)">Development of Personal Office Energy Monitor (POEM) (2011)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Development of Personal Office Energy Monitor (POEM) (2011)" data-section="40" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>As part of its efforts in the Positive Energy Buildings Consortium, Intel has been developing an application, called Personal Office Energy Monitor (POEM), to help office buildings to be more energy-efficient. With this application, employees can get the power consumption info for their office machines, so that they can figure out a better way to save energy in their working environment.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="IT_Manager_series"><i>IT Manager</i> series</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: IT Manager series" data-section="41" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/IT_Manager_3:_Unseen_Forces" title="IT Manager 3: Unseen Forces">IT Manager 3: Unseen Forces</a> and <a href="/wiki/IT_Manager:_Duels" title="IT Manager: Duels">IT Manager: Duels</a></div>
<p>Intel has introduced some simulation games, starting in 2009 with web-based <i><a href="/wiki/IT_Manager_3:_Unseen_Forces" title="IT Manager 3: Unseen Forces">IT Manager 3: Unseen Forces</a></i>. In it, the player manages a company's IT department. The goal is to apply technology and skill to enable the company to grow from a small business into a global enterprise.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (January 2018)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The game has since been discontinued and succeeded in 2012 by the web-based multiplayer game <i><a href="/wiki/IT_Manager:_Duels" title="IT Manager: Duels">IT Manager: Duels</a></i>, which is no longer available.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Car_Security_System_.282011.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Car_Security_System_(2011)">Car Security System (2011)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Car Security System (2011)" data-section="42" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2011, Intel announced that it is working on a car security system that connects to smartphones via an application. The application works by streaming video to a cloud service if a car armed with the system is broken into.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="High-Bandwidth_Digital_Content_Protection">High-Bandwidth Digital Content Protection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: High-Bandwidth Digital Content Protection" data-section="43" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Intel also developed <a href="/wiki/High-bandwidth_Digital_Content_Protection" title="High-bandwidth Digital Content Protection">High-Bandwidth Digital Content Protection (HDCP)</a> to prevent access of digital audio and video content as it travels across connections.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Move_from_intel_desktop_to_open_mobile_platforms_.282013.E2.80.932014.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Move_from_intel_desktop_to_open_mobile_platforms_(2013–2014)">Move from intel desktop to open mobile platforms (2013–2014)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Move from intel desktop to open mobile platforms (2013–2014)" data-section="44" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2013, Intel's Kirk Skaugen said that Intel's exclusive focus on Microsoft platforms was a thing of the past and that they would now support all "tier-one operating systems" such as Linux, Android, iOS, and Chrome.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup></p><p>In 2014, Intel cut thousands of employees in response to "evolving market trends",<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup> and offered to subsidize manufacturers for the extra costs involved in using Intel chips in their tablets.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Introduction_of_Haswell_processors_.282013.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction_of_Haswell_processors_(2013)">Introduction of Haswell processors (2013)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Introduction of Haswell processors (2013)" data-section="45" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Haswell_(microarchitecture)" title="Haswell (microarchitecture)">Haswell (microarchitecture)</a></div>
<p>In June 2013, Intel unveiled its fourth generation of Intel Core processors (<a href="/wiki/Haswell_(microarchitecture)" title="Haswell (microarchitecture)">Haswell</a>) in an event named Computex in <a href="/wiki/Taipei" title="Taipei">Taipei</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Wearable_fashion_.282014.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wearable_fashion_(2014)">Wearable fashion (2014)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Wearable fashion (2014)" data-section="46" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On January 6, 2014, Intel announced that it was "teaming with the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Fashion_Designers_of_America" title="Council of Fashion Designers of America">Council of Fashion Designers of America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barneys_New_York" title="Barneys New York">Barneys New York</a> and Opening Ceremony around the wearable tech field."<sup id="cite_ref-Intel_Making_Fashion_Push_Into_Wearable_Technology_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intel_Making_Fashion_Push_Into_Wearable_Technology-184">[183]</a></sup></p><p>Intel developed a reference design for <a href="/wiki/Wearable_technology" title="Wearable technology">wearable</a> smart earbuds that provide biometric and fitness information. The Intel smart earbuds provide full stereo audio, and monitor heart rate, while the applications on the user's phone keep track of run distance and calories burned.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/CNBC" title="CNBC">CNBC</a> reported that Intel eliminated the division that worked on health wearables in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Fog_computing">Fog computing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Fog computing" data-section="47" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On November 19, 2015, Intel, alongside <a href="/wiki/ARM_Holdings" class="mw-redirect" title="ARM Holdings">ARM Holdings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dell" title="Dell">Dell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cisco_Systems" title="Cisco Systems">Cisco Systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>, founded the <a href="/wiki/OpenFog_Consortium" title="OpenFog Consortium">OpenFog Consortium</a>, to promote interests and development in <a href="/wiki/Fog_computing" title="Fog computing">fog computing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup> Intel's Chief Strategist for the IoT Strategy and Technology Office, Jeff Faders, became the consortium's first president.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[186]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict-free_production">Conflict-free production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Conflict-free production" data-section="48" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2009, Intel announced that it planned to undertake an effort to remove <a href="/wiki/Conflict_resource" title="Conflict resource">conflict resources</a>—materials sourced from mines whose profits are used to fund armed militant groups, particularly within the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>—from its supply chain. Intel sought conflict-free sources of the precious metals common to electronics from within the country, using a system of first- and third-party audits, as well as input from the <a href="/wiki/Enough_Project" title="Enough Project">Enough Project</a> and other organizations. During a keynote address at <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Electronics_Show" title="Consumer Electronics Show">Consumer Electronics Show</a> 2014, Intel CEO at the time, Brian Krzanich, announced that the company's microprocessors would henceforth be conflict free. In 2016, Intel stated that it had expected its entire supply chain to be conflict-free by the end of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[189]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-driving_cars">Self-driving cars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Self-driving cars" data-section="49" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Intel is one of the biggest stakeholders in the <a href="/wiki/Self-driving_car" title="Self-driving car">self-driving car</a> industry, having joined the race in mid 2017<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup> after joining forces with Mobileye.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[191]</a></sup> The company is also one of the first in the sector to research consumer acceptance, after an AAA report quoted a 78% nonacceptance rate of the technology in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-193">[192]</a></sup></p><p>Safety levels of the technology, the thought of abandoning control to a machine, and psychological comfort of passengers in such situations were the major discussion topics initially. The commuters also stated that they did not want to see everything the car was doing. This was primarily a referral to the auto-steering wheel with no one sitting in the driving seat. Intel also learned that voice control regulator is vital, and the interface between the humans and machine eases the discomfort condition, and brings some sense of control back.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-194">[193]</a></sup> It is important to mention that Intel included only 10 people in this study, which makes the study less credible.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-193">[192]</a></sup> In a video posted on YouTube,<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[194]</a></sup> Intel accepted this fact and called for further testing.
</p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(4)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_affairs">Corporate affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Corporate affairs" data-section="50" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-4 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-4">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_and_corporate_structure">Leadership and corporate structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Leadership and corporate structure" data-section="51" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Otellini_Barrett_Maloney.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Otellini_Barrett_Maloney.jpg/220px-Otellini_Barrett_Maloney.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="735"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 162px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Otellini_Barrett_Maloney.jpg/220px-Otellini_Barrett_Maloney.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="162" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Otellini_Barrett_Maloney.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Paul Otellini, Craig Barrett and Sean Maloney (2006)</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Noyce" title="Robert Noyce">Robert Noyce</a> was Intel's CEO at its founding in 1968, followed by co-founder <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Moore" title="Gordon Moore">Gordon Moore</a> in 1975. <a href="/wiki/Andy_Grove" class="mw-redirect" title="Andy Grove">Andy Grove</a> became the company's president in 1979 and added the CEO title in 1987 when Moore became chairman. In 1998, Grove succeeded Moore as Chairman, and <a href="/wiki/Craig_Barrett_(Intel_President)" class="mw-redirect" title="Craig Barrett (Intel President)">Craig Barrett</a>, already company president, took over. On May 18, 2005, Barrett handed the reins of the company over to <a href="/wiki/Paul_Otellini" title="Paul Otellini">Paul Otellini</a>, who had been the company president and COO and who was responsible for Intel's design win in the original <a href="/wiki/IBM_PC" class="mw-redirect" title="IBM PC">IBM PC</a>. The board of directors elected Otellini as President and CEO, and Barrett replaced Grove as <a href="/wiki/Board_of_directors" title="Board of directors">Chairman of the Board</a>. Grove stepped down as chairman but is retained as a special adviser. In May 2009, Barrett stepped down as chairman of the Board and was succeeded by Jane Shaw. In May 2012, Intel vice chairman Andy Bryant, who had held the posts of CFO (1994) and Chief Administrative Officer (2007) at Intel, succeeded Shaw as executive chairman.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup></p><p>In November 2012, president and CEO Paul Otellini announced that he would step down in May 2013 at the age of 62, three years before the company's mandatory retirement age. During a six-month transition period, Intel's board of directors commenced a search process for the next CEO, in which it considered both internal managers and external candidates such as <a href="/wiki/Sanjay_Jha_(businessman)" title="Sanjay Jha (businessman)">Sanjay Jha</a> and Patrick Gelsinger.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup> Financial results revealed that, under Otellini, Intel's revenue increased by 55.8 percent (US$34.2 to 53.3 billion), while its net income increased by 46.7% (US$7.5 billion to 11 billion).<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup></p><p>On May 2, 2013, Executive Vice President and COO <a href="/wiki/Brian_Krzanich" title="Brian Krzanich">Brian Krzanich</a> was elected as Intel's sixth CEO,<sup id="cite_ref-Alexis_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexis-199">[198]</a></sup> a selection that became effective on May 16, 2013, at the company's annual meeting. Reportedly, the board concluded that an insider could proceed with the role and exert an impact more quickly, without the need to learn Intel's processes, and Krzanich was selected on such a basis.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[199]</a></sup> Intel's software head <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9e_James" title="Renée James">Renée James</a> was selected as president of the company, a role that is second to the CEO position.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup></p><p>As of May 2013, Intel's board of directors consists of Andy Bryant, John Donahoe, Frank Yeary, Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Charlene_Barshefsky" title="Charlene Barshefsky">Charlene Barshefsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Susan_Decker" title="Susan Decker">Susan Decker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reed_Hundt" title="Reed Hundt">Reed Hundt</a>, Paul Otellini, James Plummer, David Pottruck, and David Yoffie and Creative director <a href="/wiki/Will.i.am" title="Will.i.am">will.i.am</a>. The board was described by former <i>Financial Times</i> journalist Tom Foremski as "an exemplary example of corporate governance of the highest order" and received a rating of ten from GovernanceMetrics International, a form of recognition that has only been awarded to twenty-one other corporate boards worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup></p><p>On June 21, 2018, Intel announced the resignation of Brian Krzanich as CEO, with the exposure of a relationship he had with an employee. <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Swan" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert H. Swan">Bob Swan</a> was named interim CEO, as the Board began a search for a permanent CEO.
</p><p>On January 31, 2019, <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Swan" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert H. Swan">Swan</a> transitioned from his role as CFO and interim CEO and was named by the Board as the 7th CEO to lead the company.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Ownership">Ownership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Ownership" data-section="52" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>As of 2017 Intel shares are mainly held by institutional investors (<a href="/wiki/The_Vanguard_Group" title="The Vanguard Group">The Vanguard Group</a>, <a href="/wiki/BlackRock" title="BlackRock">BlackRock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capital_Group_Companies" title="Capital Group Companies">Capital Group Companies</a>, <a href="/wiki/State_Street_Corporation" title="State Street Corporation">State Street Corporation</a> and others<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup>)
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Employment">Employment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Employment" data-section="53" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Intel&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>.
